    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="740aec7dd046439f84e3bb2994ad09bd">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '9801d2ba-a550-4c45-970f-1e81a7e20f42'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d219164427" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The sodium storage behavior in the plateau region is crucial for determining the capacity and rate capability of hard carbon (HC) anodes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Key structural features for achieving excellent plateau performance include extended graphite domains and increased interlayer spacing. However, synchronously optimizing these two structures is challenging due to their inherent trade-off. Herein, a tandem catalytic carbonization strategy is developed to construct HC with long graphite domains (L<sub>a</sub> = 5.31&nbsp;nm) and large interlayer spacing (d<sub>002</sub> = 0.389&nbsp;nm) simultaneously. Comprehensive in situ and ex situ tests unravel the catalytic selective bond breaking and aromatization effects of ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, the catalytic graphitic layers enlargement and occupied effects of formed ZnO and Zn in different temperature stages, leading to the formation of the unique structure. The optimal HCZ-0.1 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 346.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a plateau capacity of 249.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and high-rate performance (114.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>). In addition, the sodium storage mechanism and origin of enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics of HCZ-0.1 are also revealed. This work offers a precise method to engineer the graphite microcrystal structure in HC for superior sodium storage in the plateau region.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202416061-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Due to the abundant sodium resources and similar working mechanisms to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are generally regarded as an important complement to the energy storage field.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0001_R_d219164419e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0002_R_d219164419e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, currently commercialized graphite anodes are not suitable for sodium battery storage due to the thermodynamic instability between sodium and graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0003_R_d219164419e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0004_R_d219164419e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, finding suitable anode materials is crucial to promote the commercialization of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0005_R_d219164419e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - eScience">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among the many available anode materials, including carbonaceous materials, alloys and metal compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0006_R_d219164419e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0009_R_d219164419e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> hard carbon (HC) materials are considered to be the most promising anode materials for high-energy-density SIBs because of their low operating potential and abundant raw material resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0010_R_d219164419e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Biomass-based HC with low economic costs and unique inherent structures have been extensively studied.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0011_R_d219164419e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Many efforts have been made to improve the performance of biomass-derived HC for sodium storage, such as expanding the interlayer spacing of graphite to achieve rapid (de)intercalation of sodium ions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0012_R_d219164419e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0013_R_d219164419e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> constructing long graphite microcrystalline regions to achieve rapid electron transport,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0014_R_d219164419e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> optimizing porous systems to promote storage of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na clusters in appropriate pores,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0015_R_d219164419e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0016_R_d219164419e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and doping heteroatoms or defects to provide more charge adsorption sites, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0017_R_d219164419e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although a lot of progress has been made in current work, the performance of HC is still not satisfactory for practical applications.</p>
         
         <p>Generally, the typical charge-discharge curve of HC is shown to be characterized by a high potential slope (&gt;0.10&nbsp;V) and a low potential plateau (0.01–0.10&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0018_R_d219164419e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium storage in the sloping region can be largely attributed to adsorption, the performance of which is closely related to defect concentration and open pore volumes. While in the plateau region, intercalation and pore filling are the main mechanisms for sodium storage. The graphite microcrystal structure and closed pores play key roles in determining the sodium storage performance of HC in this region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0019_R_d219164419e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0020_R_d219164419e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In some sense, sodium adsorption in the sloping regions can be classified as physical energy storage, although it has fast kinetics, its contribution to the whole capacity of HC is relatively small.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0021_R_d219164419e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energy Storage Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, high sloping capacity often accompanied by low initial coulombic efficiency (ICE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0022_R_d219164419e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the insertion and pore filling of sodium in plateau region will result in high capacity without sacrificing ICE, but the relatively slow kinetics often lead to poor rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0023_R_d219164419e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Accordingly, to pursue high whole capacity and good rate performance of HC, it is crucial to improve the sodium storage performance at the plateau region.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0024_R_d219164419e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To boost the plateau performance, strategies that can expand interlayer spacing, enlarge the graphite domains, and increase the closed pores number are often adopted.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0025_R_d219164419e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0026_R_d219164419e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Bai et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0023_R_d219164419e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> enlarged the carbon interlayer spacing by doping higher pyridinic nitrogen, reduced the energy barrier of the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process, and improved the plateau capacity. Chou et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0027_R_d219164419e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> utilized manganese ions to assist in the catalytic carbonization process to precisely regulate the degree of graphitization, obtaining long-range graphite domains that facilitate the fast transmission of electrons and closed pores formation. Hou et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0028_R_d219164419e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> dispersed single atom Ni on the carbon skeleton and used a coordination modification strategy to adjust the pore structure and increase the number of closed pores through the structural evolution of Ni-NPCs at different sodium storage stages to improve the capacity. Despite the encouraging achievements obtained above, isolated efforts are of not much value for their effects, and HC with superior plateau performances are still urgently needed. Theoretically, an ideal HC with excellent plateau performances should integrate the structure characteristics of long graphite domains, large interlayer spacing and many closed pores. Nevertheless, it is still challenging to precisely regulate these structures synchronously because of their trade-off relationship. In general, activation, doping and reducing carbonization temperature can expand the interlayer spacing of HC, but they also result in a decrease in graphite domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0029_R_d219164419e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano Res.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, high-temperature carbonization and metal-meditated catalytic graphitization are beneficial for the formation of long graphite domains, but will also lead to compression of the interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0030_R_d219164419e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is urgent to develop an effective approach that can directionally regulate the graphite microcrystal structure of HC for the joint improvement of interlayer spacing and graphite domains.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0031_R_d219164419e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a zinc salts-assisted tandem catalytic carbonization method to precisely modulate the micro-nano structure for constructing HC with both large interlayers spacing and long graphitic domains. The unique structure of the prepared HCs has been fully characterized and verified by various techniques. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman, thermogravimetry-infrared spectroscopy (TG-IR) and ex situ electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) etc. tests were performed to unravel the tandem catalytic carbonization mechanism. The sodium storage performance of prepared HCs was studied. Furthermore, the sodium storage mechanism and origin of enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics were studied and revealed by galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT), density functional theory (DFT) and in situ electrochemical Raman tests. Finally, the electrochemical performance of the optimum HC as the anode in a full cell was investigated. The results demonstrate the effectiveness of tandem catalytic carbonization strategy for targeted construction of HC with long graphite domains and large interlayer spacing, allowing for high plateau capacity and good rate capability. This work enables accurately regulating the graphite microcrystal structure of HC and is expected to contribute to the development of promise anode for the emerging SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202416061-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202416061-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation and Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Bamboo, chosen as the precursor in this work, is a widely available renewable natural resource. Since bamboo has a certain degree of cross-linking and its fiber crystallinity is higher than that of traditional wood, it is suitable for the preparation of HC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0032_R_d219164419e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. J.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0033_R_d219164419e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Commun. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synthesis of HC form biomass catalyzed by ZnCl<sub>2</sub>-assisted is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1a</a>. The bamboo was first impregnated with ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, and then different thermal stages were connected in series, including pyrolysis at 600 °C for 2&nbsp;h and pyrolysis at 1300 °C for 2&nbsp;h, to finally obtain the target HC. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) characterized the morphology of the sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). HCZ-0.1 presents a typical HC structure, and chaotically stacked graphene sheets were shown. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images show that HCZ-0.1 presents long-range graphene channels with a layer spacing (d<sub>002</sub>) of 0.389&nbsp;nm. HC without activation treatment showed a more disordered structure with a similar interlayer distance (0.371&nbsp;nm) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1b,c</a>). Different concentrations of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> were used to activate bamboo, and it was found that HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15 exhibited different lengths of graphite domains (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/33e318d8-574b-49fc-b3f4-539fdb7c8f1a/adfm202416061-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/33e318d8-574b-49fc-b3f4-539fdb7c8f1a/adfm202416061-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ade5e195-5c48-40e3-a3f0-f797f5697d12/adfm202416061-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/33e318d8-574b-49fc-b3f4-539fdb7c8f1a/adfm202416061-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis procedure. b, c) TEM image of HC and HCZ-0.1 (insets are the calculated interlayer distance, as well as enlarged image based on yellow box). d) XRD patterns, e) Physical parameters of samples, f) FT-IR spectra of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>XRD results show that in HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15, broad diffraction peaks of amorphous carbon are detected at around 24° (002) and 43° (100) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1d</a>). As the ZnCl<sub>2</sub> content increases, the (002) peak position moves to different angles, indicating changes in the interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0034_R_d219164419e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The physical parameters of the graphite domains of several HC samples were calculated based on XRD.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0035_R_d219164419e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Environ. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The average domain length (La) of HCZ-0.1 is 5.31&nbsp;nm and the graphite domain thickness (Lc) is 2.57&nbsp;nm. In contrast, HC exhibits a more disordered structure, La (≈2.23&nbsp;nm), Lc (≈1.07&nbsp;nm) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that without the addition of ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, graphene sheets have a relatively narrow interlayer spacing and are randomly stacked, which is a normal HC morphology. As the concentration of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> increases, the length and stacking thickness of lamellar graphene first increase and then decrease, while the interlayer spacing does not change much. ZnCl<sub>2</sub> catalyzes the reorganization of the carbon layer toward long-range order while maintaining large interlayer spacing. The La (≈1.62&nbsp;nm) and Lc (≈0.78&nbsp;nm) of HCZ-0.15 indicate that excessive ZnCl<sub>2</sub> treatment can cause the ZnO generated at high temperature to over-etch the graphite domain during the graphitization process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1e</a>). The molecular structure of the precursor significantly determines its thermal evolution process. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) was used to study the changes in functional groups during the pyrolysis of bamboo into carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0018_R_d219164419e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are obvious characteristic peaks at about 1630 and 1120 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which correspond to the asymmetric vibration of C═C and C─O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1f</a>). The characteristic peaks of the C═O group in HCZ-0.1 and HCZ-0.15 were weakened, indicating that the C═O bond was broken after ZnCl<sub>2</sub> treatment.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed on the surface structure of the material, and the overall spectrum is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information. The high-resolution C 1 s spectrum can be divided into three peaks, including C-C (sp<sup>2</sup>, 284.1&nbsp;eV), C-C (sp<sup>3</sup>, 285.1&nbsp;eV), C-O (286.5&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0036_R_d219164419e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Res.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The proportion of C-C (sp<sup>2</sup>) in the total area of HCZ-0.1 is higher than that of HC. The reduction of sp<sup>3</sup> hybrid carbon confirmed that ZnCl<sub>2</sub>-catalyzed sample to increase the aromatization of carbon (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2a</a>). The specific surface area and pore structure of several samples were investigated through N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0025_R_d219164419e723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2b</a>, HC has a large specific surface area (46.3 m<sup>2</sup>·g<sup>−1</sup>). With the addition of ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, the specific surface area decreases, and HCZ-0.1 has the smallest specific surface area of 5.77 m<sup>2</sup>·g<sup>−1</sup>. After activation of too much ZnCl<sub>2</sub>, the specific surface area of HCZ-0.15 increased slightly by 9.7 m<sup>2</sup>·g<sup>−1</sup>. All materials show a large number of micropores (&lt;2&nbsp;nm), among which the distribution of micropores (&gt;1 nm) and ultra-micropores (&lt;1&nbsp;nm) is different, which may be related to the change of pore structure induced by ZnCl<sub>2</sub> during heat treatment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2c</a>). Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) is considered a powerful technique to analyze the closed pore features that cannot be detected by N<sub>2</sub>. A shoulder peak with the q range of 0.05–0.4 Å<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the closed pores of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0037_R_d219164419e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Storage Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HCZ-0.1 shows the strongest signals in this range, indicating it has the most abundant closed pores among the prepared HCs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0025_R_d219164419e767" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This structure of HC also exhibits the best electrical conductivity (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Raman spectroscopy was performed on different samples, 1350 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to defective carbon, and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to crystalline carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0038_R_d219164419e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the ZnCl<sub>2</sub> concentration increased, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio changed. HCZ-0.1 shows the smallest <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> (1.12), indicating that it has the highest degree of order, which is consistent with TEM that long-range graphene results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2e</a>). The mapping of Raman also obtained consistent results. For HCZ-0.1, the carbon surface has uniform defects and the graphitic carbon layer is ordered (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0002">2f</a>). A certain concentration of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> can improve the order degree of carbon. Excessively high concentrations of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> also cause further increases in defects, which may be caused by excessive body etching. Controlling the concentration of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> can change the degree of catalytic graphitization of carbon at high temperatures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a292b83e-450d-434a-b59c-07676c23a4fa/adfm202416061-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a292b83e-450d-434a-b59c-07676c23a4fa/adfm202416061-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0d2618e0-2dce-475b-aa86-d2a492ad59d3/adfm202416061-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a292b83e-450d-434a-b59c-07676c23a4fa/adfm202416061-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Fitted high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15. b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15. c) Pore size distribution of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1 and HCZ-0.15. d) Small-angle X-ray scattering patterns. e) Raman spectra, f) Raman mapping of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15.</div>
                  </figcaption>

            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202416061-sec-0040-title">2.2 Catalytic Carbonization Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>The different evolution statuses of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> in catalytic carbonization will affect the crystallites and pore structure of HC. The structural changes during carbonization were analyzed by TG-IR, Raman and XRD, ex situ electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) etc. In situ IR at different temperatures of HCZ-0.1 showed that different gas peaks appeared at 200–300 °C. H<sub>2</sub>O was mainly released at 200–300 °C, and a large amount of CO/CO<sub>2</sub> was released at 300–600 °C (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3a</a>). This process shows that C-O and C-H were largely broken, H and O were eliminated, and carbon crystallites gradually appeared.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0039_R_d219164419e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, without ZnCl<sub>2</sub>-mediated HC, the peak intensity of CO/CO<sub>2</sub> is relatively larger and the peak intensity of H<sub>2</sub>O is slightly weaker, which proves that ZnCl<sub>2</sub> has an excellent catalytic selective bond-breaking effect (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3b</a>). In addition, the thermogravimetric (TG) analysis showed that a slight mass loss was observed in the range from the initial temperature to 200 °C, corresponding to the evaporation of water (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3c</a>). A severe mass loss was observed in the range from 200 to 400 °C, at which time most of the hydrogen and oxygen were removed, and the free carbon atoms underwent intramolecular ring formation and intermolecular aromatization reactions, eventually forming carbon microcrystals. The appropriate concentration of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> had the best effect, which was manifested by the presence of a preferential decline peak in HCZ-0.1, corresponding to the faster water loss in DTG (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). It proves that the appropriate amount of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> catalyzes the rapid hydrolysis of the precursor with minimal carbon loss. Elemental analysis showed that the carbon fixation rate of HCZ-0.1 was the best reaching 28.9% (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). At 600 °C, on one hand, HCZ-0.1 has more sp<sup>2</sup> carbons, and the number of oxygen-containing functional groups is significantly less than that of HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3d</a>). The presence of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon helps to promote the growth of long La in HCZ-0.1 during the pyrolysis and rearrangement of carbon in the high-temperature refining stage. On the other hand, due to ZnCl<sub>2</sub> catalyzes the hydrolysis of large amounts of precursors and reduces their cross-linking degree, HCZ-0.1 has a larger specific surface area in 600 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3cee1c01-445e-4cc0-901c-c84939a1697e/adfm202416061-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3cee1c01-445e-4cc0-901c-c84939a1697e/adfm202416061-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9265b4e1-9743-4c0c-9008-7cde69044d18/adfm202416061-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3cee1c01-445e-4cc0-901c-c84939a1697e/adfm202416061-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Typical IR spectra of the gaseous products of HCZ-0.1, and b) HC. c) TG of different precursors. d) C 1s XPS spectra at 600 °C. e) EPR spectra of different precursors at 25 °C. f) EPR spectra carbonized at 600 °C. g) Raman spectra at different calcination temperatures. h) XRD patterns at different calcination temperatures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>At the same time, free radicals play an important role in the formation of certain HC structures, so ex situ EPR is used to characterize the free radical concentration of different samples. As can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3e,f</a>, the intensity and position of the spectrum change, indicating that the participation of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> has a certain effect on the formation and existence of free radicals in bamboo.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0040_R_d219164419e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Powder Technol.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After pretreatment of biomass materials, the <i>g</i>-value deviates from the free mobile electron (<i>g</i> = 2.0023), indicating that it gradually tends to be free radicals with C/O mixed centers (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0041_R_d219164419e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nat. Commun.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The free radical concentration of HCZ-0.1 is significantly increased compared with HC at 600 °C, providing a favorable environment for the formation of graphite microcrystals. The free radicals of HCZ-0.1 show a very narrow peak width, which is very similar to the results of materials such as graphite oxide and oxidized CNT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3f</a>). This type of free radical is confined to certain positions (more localized), often existing in sigma dangling bonds at the edge of the benzene ring structure, such as carbon defects and oxygen-containing functional groups at the edge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0042_R_d219164419e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The free radicals of HC are derived from the extended aromatic structure with a more delocalized nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0043_R_d219164419e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Fuel Process. Technol.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This peak width indicates that the spin interaction of the free radicals is very fast.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0044_R_d219164419e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Appl. Magn. Reson.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has a relatively complete graphene-like structure, which is not conducive to the formation of closed pores during the subsequent high-temperature refining process. Simply, the location of the free radicals implies that they play different roles during the high-temperature refining process. The free radicals in HC at 600 °C exist inside the graphene structure and contribute to the formation of C═C. The free radicals in HCZ-0.1 at 600 °C exist in the edge of the structure, which is conducive to participating in the formation of oxygen bridge bonds such as C-O-C, promoting the folding effect of the graphite microcrystalline, thereby expanding the interlayer spacing. Moreover, the free radicals at the edge will have an inductive effect and will preferentially participate in the bonding process, making it easier for key structures such as closed pores to form.</p>
            
            <p>In situ Raman at different calcination temperatures was used to collect fixed-point spectral data of HCZ-0.1 (Figure <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3g</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supping Information). Between 400 and 600 °C, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value changes from small to large, proving that carbon microcrystals gradually nucleated at this stage, which also corresponds to the large weight loss of mass at 400 °C in TG. At 800–1000 °C, the intensity of both D and G peaks increased significantly and the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio was still getting larger, proving that the carbon microcrystals were disordered and small in size at this time. This phenomenon is believed to be related to the formation of ZnO at high temperatures, which is confirmed by the results of XRD patterns at different calcination temperatures (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0003">3h</a>).</p>
            
            <p>Based on the above analysis, the carbon formation mechanism of HCZ-x based on the tandem catalytic carbonization method has been proposed (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0004">4</a>). In low-temperature ranges (≤600 °C), the catalytic selective bond breaking and aromatization effects of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> promote the dehydration and condensation of biomass precursors, leaving more carbon free radicals for the formation of long-range graphitic layers. In middle-temperature ranges (600–900 °C), zinc ions combine with oxygen-containing functional groups to generate partial ZnO, which etches carbon and creates more micropores, providing favorable conditions for the transfer of sodium ions. Interestingly, with the increase of carbonization temperature, the interlayer spacing theoretically decreases gradually, but the interlayer spacing does not change significantly when the temperature reaches 700–900 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). These phenomena prove that elemental zinc plays an occupying and supporting role, thereby avoiding the compression of interlayer spacing. In high-temperature ranges (900–1300 °C), there is only a typical carbon peak and larger interlayer spacing and graphite domain size at 1300 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0001">1d,e</a>), indicating that the formed zinc catalyzes the growth of graphite layers and the conversion of micropores to closed pores, and maintains a large interlayer spacing. Therefore, as the carbonization temperature changes, the different forms of zinc generated in situ play different roles in the biomass carbonization process. So far, the above changes in microstructure have proved that ZnCl<sub>2</sub> tandem catalysis can achieve the adjustment of pseudo-graphite domains including interlayer spacing, grain size and carbon hybridization state of carbon.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/381c68e5-dfa7-44ad-b733-a18a09ccb0c0/adfm202416061-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/381c68e5-dfa7-44ad-b733-a18a09ccb0c0/adfm202416061-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0a2c842f-43e6-4c2a-ac31-34be61c7638e/adfm202416061-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/381c68e5-dfa7-44ad-b733-a18a09ccb0c0/adfm202416061-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Carbon formation mechanism of HCZ-x.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202416061-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance of Half-Cells</h3>
            
            <p>A series of electrochemical tests were performed to evaluate the relationship between the structure of carbon and electrochemical performance. The electrochemical properties of the samples were revealed by Na-HC half-cell using a conventional ester-based electrolyte (1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in the electrolyte). First, cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were performed in the voltage range of 0–2.0&nbsp;V. The cycle values of the first three cycles were intercepted at a current density of 0.05A g<sup>−1</sup>. The CV curves overlapped after the second cycle, showing that the sample has excellent reversibility (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5a</a>). The initial three cycles of the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curve of HCZ-0.1 at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5b</a>, in which the reversible specific capacity output by HCZ-0.1 is 346.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, the HCZ-0.1 electrode exhibits 74% of the highest ICE. High ICE may be related to high graphitization degree and large interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0024_R_d219164419e1058" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nano Energy">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The GCD curves of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15 electrodes were obtained at a current density of 0.05A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5c</a>). HCZ-0.1 has the highest reversible capacity of 346.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, while HC, HCZ-0.05, and HCZ-0.15 only provide reversible capacity of 306.4, 318.3, and 336.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. To study the charge storage dynamics in depth, the charge–discharge curve of the sample was divided into two regions, including the low potential plateau region (0.01−0.10&nbsp;V) and the sloping region (0.10–2.00&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0045_R_d219164419e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HCZ-0.1 has the best plateau capacity (249.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), which is higher than HC (125.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), HCZ-0.05 (227.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and HCZ-0.15 (241.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), HCZ-0.1 contributed a larger plateau capacity of 72% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2a9581bf-932f-4639-aaaa-63cd1c469b03/adfm202416061-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2a9581bf-932f-4639-aaaa-63cd1c469b03/adfm202416061-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/beec60f5-954a-4bc9-9495-734fbb24af69/adfm202416061-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2a9581bf-932f-4639-aaaa-63cd1c469b03/adfm202416061-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the first three cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> of HCZ-0.1. b) GCD curves of the first three cycles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> of HCZ-0.1. c) GCD profiles at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, d) Sloping/plateau capacity contribution comparison, e) Rate capability of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15. f) CV of HCZ-0.1 at different scan speeds. g) The Calculated b value of HCZ-0.1. h) Capacitance contribution rate at different scan speeds. i) Cycling stability at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> of HCZ-0.1.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In addition, rate and cycle tests were conducted on the sample. The charging capacity of the HCZ-0.1 electrode was excellent at all rates. Highly reversible charge capacities of 346.9, 278.5, 250.3, 227.5, 204.6, 187.2, 137.1, 114.0, and 271.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. When returning to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity retention rate reaches 97%. HCZ-0.1 has ultrafast Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetic in the range of 0.05 to 8 A g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5e</a>). The behavior of the capacitor is likely to support the rapid electrochemical reaction. CV was used to measure the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> at different scan rates, with the voltage ranging from 0 to 2.0&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5f</a>). And calculate the corresponding b value of HCZ-0.1 according to the formula (i = a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>), where i is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan speed, and a and b are constants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0046_R_d219164419e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathodic peak b value (b<sub>C</sub>) of HCZ-0.1 is 0.774, and the anode peak b value (b<sub>A</sub>) is 0.626, both close to 1, indicating that a large part of the sodium storage process in HCZ-0.1 is affected by the capacitance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5g</a>). At the same time, based on i (<i>v</i>) = k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0047_R_d219164419e1166" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the capacitive and battery behavior in HCZ-0.1 were quantitatively analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5h</a>). With the increase in scanning times, the proportion of capacitive behavior gradually increases, and HCZ-0.1 exhibits faster electrochemical kinetics, which is also the main reason for its excellent rate performance. The capacity retention was 90% (305.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the HCZ-0.1 electrode has excellent cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0005">5i</a>). This high plateau capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability are superior to state-of-the-art anode materials reported, indicating that HCZ-0.1 is a promising anode material for SIBs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>,&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202416061-sec-0060-title">2.4 Na-Ion Storage Kinetics Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>To further quantify the sodium storage kinetics of HCZ-0.1, various test assays were performed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0048_R_d219164419e1200" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) during the sodiation and desodiation processes was measured using GITT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0049_R_d219164419e1211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Environ. Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values of all samples did not show significant changes in the sloping region, they all showed a turning point in the plateau region (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6a</a>). During sodiation, HCZ-0.1 exhibits the highest D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value of 10<sup>−9.65</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in the sloping region, which slightly decreases to 10<sup>−10.70</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> in the plateau region. In comparison, the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value of HC dropped from 10<sup>−9.94</sup> to 10<sup>−11.38</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6b</a> shows the difference in D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values between the plateau and sloping regions for several samples, during in desodiation. D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> is significantly enhanced in HCZ-0.1, especially in the plateau region, which may be the reason for its unprecedented kinetic performance. The higher diffusion coefficient was exhibited throughout the sodiation/desodiation process, which can be attributed to the sufficient sodium storage sites and diffusion channels provided by its superior microcrystalline structure. HCZ-0.1 exhibits incredible enhancement of D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> value in the whole procedure compared to other samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating that tandem catalysis of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> on bamboo is beneficial for the construction of advanced structure, which can be held accountable for its unprecedented rate performance with fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The Nyquist diagram also confirmed this point (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The obvious drop in impedance reflects that HCZ-0.1 has better electronic conductivity.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a82596df-ca13-4295-8609-8c856b3da02c/adfm202416061-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a82596df-ca13-4295-8609-8c856b3da02c/adfm202416061-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/128dfa51-5877-482f-a9d7-6cd950e01f39/adfm202416061-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a82596df-ca13-4295-8609-8c856b3da02c/adfm202416061-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GITT of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15 during the sodiation. b) GITT of HC, HCZ-0.05, HCZ-0.1, and HCZ-0.15 during the desodiation. c) Adsorption energies of HC, HCZ-0.1. d) Diffusion paths of Na+ in HC, HCZ-0.1. e) In situ Raman pattern and discharge curve of the HCZ-0.1 anode. f) The corresponding in situ Raman contour pattern during the discharge process. g) Schematic diagram of the sodium storage stages of HCZ-0.1.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Subsequently, DFT was calculated to reveal the mechanism of sodium storage kinetics of HCZ-0.1.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0050_R_d219164419e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a>, <a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0051_R_d219164419e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Models of graphitic carbon (GC) and defective carbon (DC) structures were built using a 5 × 5 × 1 supercell configuration respectively, and a 10&nbsp;µm vacuum was introduced on the graphene surface to minimize interlayer interactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0013_R_d219164419e1304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, GC is assumed to be an HC structure with more long-range order after regulation, while DC is considered to be unmodified HC generated at high temperatures. Adsorption energy of material for Na<sup>+</sup> was calculated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6c</a>). The smaller DC adsorption energy shows that Na<sup>+</sup> is more inclined to be adsorbed at defective sites, corresponding to better sloping capacity. Bilayer graphene sheets (bilayer GC (BGC) and bilayer DC (BDC)) were constructed to study the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the structural model, the barrier energy for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the two models was calculated. The diffusion energy barrier of HCZ-0.1 is lower than that of HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6d</a>), indicating that the modified long-range graphene is conducive to the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. The ordered structure of carbon provides conditions for rapid sodium transfer, which is consistent with the enhanced electrochemical performance of HCZ-0.1 mentioned above.</p>
            
            <p>To further confirm the structural changes and sodium storage mechanism of HCZ-0.1, in situ Raman analysis was used. During the sodiation process, the D band peak gradually broadened and decreased from 2 to 1.5&nbsp;V, but the peak position remained unchanged, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> at this stage was mainly controlled by adsorption (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6e,f</a>). For the G band peak, its position remained unchanged from 2 to 0.15&nbsp;V during the discharge process. When reaching the plateau voltage region, the G band peak gradually red-shifts from 0.15 to 0&nbsp;V, and then gradually shifts back from 0 to 0.15&nbsp;V during the charging process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). So, the sodium is inserted into the carbon layer and fills the carbon nanopores. It is due to the weakening and elongation of the C-C bond caused by the transfer of electrons to the π* antibonding band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0052_R_d219164419e1341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the diffusion coefficient in GITT first decreased and then increased in the range of 0.15–0&nbsp;V, which also indicated that there were two behaviors in the pore filling process. Combining GITT and in situ Raman analysis, the sodium storage mechanism can be described in the schematic diagram of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0006">6g</a>. During the sodiation process, the sloping region is mainly dominated by the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> on the active centers on the surface or defects, and the plateau region is dominated by the interlayer insertion dominated process and the pore filling dominated process.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202416061-sec-0070-title">2.5 Electrochemical Performance of Full Cell</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the practical application of HC materials in full cells, a coin cell was assembled as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0007">7a</a>. Using HCZ-0.1 as the anode electrode, NVP as the cathode electrode, and carbonate lipids as the electrolyte, the test was conducted in the voltage range of 1.5–3.9&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0053_R_d219164419e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Energy Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The capacity ratio of the negative electrode to the positive electrode (N/P) is in the range of 1.1–1.2 to ensure maximum energy density and stability of the Na-ion full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202416061-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202416061-bib-0054_R_d219164419e1373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The GCD curves of HCZ-0.1//NVP at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0007">7b</a>. A high reversible capacity of 178.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was provided, and the capacity is normalized by the active mass of the anode electrode. Encouragingly, its average operating potential is 3.28&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0007">7b</a> inset shows the assembled full cell that can power a light-emitting diode (LED), demonstrating its technological feasibility for energy storage applications. Rate performance tests indicate superior kinetics of the HCZ-0.1//NVP SIB with a high capacity of 130.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0007">7c</a>). Furthermore, the stable capacity after 300 cycles is 153.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (capacity retention 95%) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202416061-fig-0007">7d</a>). Additionally, the fabricated coin full-cell HCZ-0.1//NVP displays a high energy density of 308.2&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Overall, the achieved properties demonstrate the excellent kinetics and stability of the fabricated HCZ-0.1//NVP system.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202416061-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b93844f-e52e-41b9-b0fe-1fdf541ebc37/adfm202416061-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b93844f-e52e-41b9-b0fe-1fdf541ebc37/adfm202416061-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f073c34e-bb95-4328-9b26-b20b52568442/adfm202416061-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b93844f-e52e-41b9-b0fe-1fdf541ebc37/adfm202416061-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202416061-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202416061" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic of sodium-ion full-cell with HCZ-0.1&nbsp;anode and NVP cathode. b) Charge–discharge curves of HCZ-0.1//NVP SIB at various current densities. The inset shows an LED lighted by a simple HCZ-0.1//NVP cell. c) Rate performance of HCZ-0.1//NVP. d) Cycling performance of HCZ-0.1//NVP SIB at the current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202416061-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202416061-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, HC with both long-range graphite layers (L<sub>a</sub> = 5.31&nbsp;nm) and large interlayer spacing (d<sub>002</sub> = 0.389&nbsp;nm) has been constructed via an innovative tandem catalytic carbonization strategy. The catalytic carbonization mechanism has also been systematically investigated through in situ XRD, Raman, TG, IR, and ex situ EPR etc. In low-temperature ranges (≤600 °C), the catalytic selective bond breaking and aromatization effects of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> promote the dehydration and condensation of biomass precursors, leaving more carbon free radicals and providing more seeds for the formation of long-range graphitic layers. In middle-temperature ranges (600–900 °C), the formed ZnO plays a role in etching graphite microcrystals to create micropores. In high-temperature ranges (900–1300 °C), the formed Zn metal catalyzed the growth of graphitic layers and the conversion of micropores to closed pores and maintained the large interlayer spacing. Owing to the special structure, the optimum HCZ-0.1 shows the high reversible capacity of 346.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and an excellent rate capability of 114.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is more than ten times that of control sample HC. Besides, combined with in situ Raman, GITT analysis and DFT study, an “adsorption-intercalation-pore filling” sodium storage mechanism has been verified. Meanwhile, the dilated interlayer spacing and enlarged graphite layers are also confirmed to be the origin of enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics. In addition, a high reversible capacity of 178.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was demonstrated at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the full cell. This work provides an effective approach to tailor the graphite microcrystal structure of HC for outstanding sodium storage at low-potential plateau regions, and is expected to contribute to the development of promise anode for the emerging SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adfm202416061-bib-0002"><span class="bullet">2</span><span class="author">S. Li</span>, <span class="author">K. Wang</span>, <span class="author">G.
